Member States, the Secretary-General/High Representative and the Commission are in contact with human rights organisations.

 

Questions to the Commission
Question no 57 by Torben Lund (H-0125/01)
 Subject: BSE and safety inspections
 

Food safety in Europe is inadequate. Time and time again, new cases of BSE are confirmed, and the authorities have been hard put to halt the spread of the disease, even though rules have been introduced on the removal and destruction of specified risk material (SRM) to protect consumers from infected meat.

The Report on Implementation of Latest BSE Control Measures in the Member States notes that one Member State has landfilled untreated BSE material and other Member States are exporting their SRM waste. Untreated BSE material could thus spread unchecked as a result.

In view of this, and given that not all Member States have the same incentives to ensure effective implementation, inspection and destruction, will the Commission explain what measures, in addition to requiring reports from Member States' national bodies, it intends to take to ensure that the Member States implement the rules effectively?

 
  
 

It is thanks to the Community measures that have been put in place that more cases of BSE are being discovered. If Member States fully apply the ban on feeding of MBM to ruminants, the removal of SRMs and are vigilant in their surveillance programmes, then BSE can be brought under control in the EU.

The implementation of Community legislation on BSE by Member States is monitored by the Food and Veterinary Office of the Commission. This monitoring takes the form of on the spot inspections in the Member States involving examination of premises, facilities, protocols and records. In response to the developments in BSE in the latter part of 2000, this programme of inspections has been stepped up. A new series of missions to the Member States will start in March 2001 in order to verify the implementation of the legislation recently adopted by the Council and which came into force on 1 January 2001. In relation to matters of public and animal health, the Commission’s policy is to maintain a high level of vigilance in order to ensure that Member States fully comply with their obligations under Community law.

At the January 2001 Council of Agriculture Ministers meeting, the Commission presented a report on the implementation of BSE control measures in the Member States. This report made reference to the fact that some Member States are having to store material pending an increase in their rendering or incineration capacity and that other Member States have to export SRM to neighbouring States for processing.

In cases where the sudden increase in material for destruction has meant that existing processing facilities are insufficient to cope with the volume, it is reasonable and unavoidable that Member States should store processed and unprocessed material pending an increase in their capacity. Providing that this storage is under official control and is performed in a hygienic manner, there is no reason that it should pose a risk to health. Similarly, the export of high risk material to other Member States for incineration is provided for in Community legislation and is subject to strict monitoring by Member States of origin and of destination.

 

Question no 58 by Mihail Papayannakis (H-0012/01)
 Subject: Radiation emissions from mobile telephones
 

Recently the British Government decided to distribute an information leaflet with each mobile telephone sold warning mobile phone users, especially young ones, of their potential health risks, since to date there is no scientific certainty as to the likelihood of health risks for users, or their extent.

The studies carried out by the Commission on the effects of electromagnetic radiation from mobile telephones on human health did not reveal any negative effects, but in spite of this some additional information is needed to reassure the public. In addition, as of September it has been compulsory in the USA for all telephones to be labelled with the radiation levels they emit. In view of the above, does the Commission intend, in order to ensure a high level of consumer protection and on the basis of the precautionary principle, to require manufacturing companies to label telephones with radiation levels or to issue some warning as to the possible harmful consequences on the brain of the use of these telephones by very young individuals? Does the Commission plan to publicise the results of the studies it has funded and, with a view to transparency, to provide information to European citizens on a regular basis?

 
  
 

The honourable Member raises the important issue of how public health and new technologies interact as regards in particular the expanding use of mobile telephones. Let me first say that over the past 20 years, there has been an important research effort under the leadership of the World Health Organisation’s electromagnetic fields project. The International Commission on Non-Ionising Radiation Protection has also been involved in this project to look for evidence of adverse health effects from the exposure to electromagnetic fields.

The Council Recommendation of 1999 on limitation of exposure of the general public to electromagnetic fields is based on the guidelines developed by the International Commission on Non-Ionising Radiation Protection, which were endorsed by the Commission’s Scientific Committee on Toxicity, Eco-Toxicity and the Environment. The Committee stated that these international guidelines provide the appropriate basis to develop exposure limits against public health risks.

Regarding long-term effects, the best available scientific knowledge does not provide sufficient evidence to conclude that long-term effects occur as a consequence of exposure to electromagnetic fields. However, taking into account the growing public concern of long-term effects of such exposure and the rapid development of the telecommunications sector, the limits set up in the Council Recommendation include an additional safety factor of about 50 to the threshold values defined by the International Commission for Non-Ionising Radiation Protection for the general public. In this way, the Recommendation would cover possible long-term effects in the whole frequency range. As a result, the guidelines in the Council Recommendation provide protection thresholds with respect to adverse health effects which may be caused by exposure, taking into consideration the variety of possible physical characteristics and exposure scenarios.

In order to cover any new scientific findings and to ensure that the scientific basis, on which the Council Recommendation is based is still valid, the Scientific Steering Committee will give an up-dated opinion in June 2001.

To ensure a uniform level of protection throughout the European Union, the Commission has mandated the standardisation bodies (CENELEC and ETSI) to work on establishing European standards, including harmonised methods of calculation and measurement. This will enable verification that the safe exposure limits set in the Recommendation are met. A first standard for mobile telephones is expected to be available in June 2001, and other standards will follow.

Research efforts on electromagnetic fields and their effects continue at international level. Within the Fifth Framework Programme for Research and Development (Quality of Life programme, Key Action “Environment & Health”), the European Commission funds EUR 9 million of research on the potential carcinogenic effects of radio frequencies. The Commission is supporting the largest study ever conducted on possible carcinogenic effects of electromagnetic fields. This study is coordinated by the International Association for Research on Cancer (IARC). Results are expected by 2004. This research is directly applicable to the protection of the public and will lead to strengthening the scientific basis for the establishment of exposure limit values.

 

Question no 59 by Jean-Louis Bernié (H-0082/01)
 Subject: Bee deaths resulting from insecticide use
 

The use of insecticides based on imidacloprid (Gaucho) and fipronil (Regent), both of which are highly toxic to bees, is creating a disastrous economic situation for French beekeepers.

The situation is particularly serious as in the areas where the insecticides are used bee stocks have been decimated. These insecticides also cause serious environmental damage which must be considered as a matter of urgency. Imidacloprid and fipronil enter the soil and contaminate harvests from one year to the next, even if seed has not been treated. Furthermore, the disturbing bee mortality rate could pose a risk to the flora-fauna equilibrium.

Is the Commission intending to ban the use of imidacloprid and fipronil, which have such disastrous environmental effects?

 
  
 

The use of plant protection products in the Community is governed by Council Directive 91/414/EEC on the placing of plant protection products on the market. The Directive stipulates that the evaluation of the active substances should be done at Community level but that the specific evaluations and authorisations of products containing evaluated substances should be done by the Member States.

The active substances fipronil and imidacloprid were already on the market at the entry into force of the Directive and therefore they have to be re­evaluated at Community level. These active substances are included respectively in the second and third phase of the review programme for existing active substances. For both active substances industry has committed to submit a dossier in order to demonstrate that there are acceptable uses in accordance with Article 5 of the Directive. The Commission will report to Parliament and the Council next July on its progress with the re­evaluation programme.

Member States can only grant authorisations provided that the plant protection product, when properly used, does not have, inter alia, an unacceptable effect on the environment. As part of this assessment the impact on non­target insects, including honeybees, has to be evaluated. If necessary, appropriate risk mitigation measures such as use­restrictions need to be put in place.

For imidacloprid, the damage to bees resulting from use on sunflowers has not been proven but, since the currently available scientific evidence is not conclusive, the French authorities have suspended the use of imidacloprid on sunflowers. An epidemiological study will be carried out over the next 2 years. The Commission is not aware of similar problems in other Member States.

For fipronil the French authorities evaluated recently new information which did not show concerns on honeybees.

The Commission will follow further developments but does not intend, in view of the currently available information, to ban the use of any of the substances mentioned.

 

Question no 60 by Inger Schörling (H-0128/01)
 Subject: Transport of animals
 

All over Europe, people are shocked by the pictures they see in the press and on television showing how animals are treated in the EU's industrial farming sector. Commissioner Byrne is on record as saying that he personally is disgusted by the way animals are being tortured, although he excused himself by saying that the EU does not have sufficient power to be able to do anything to about it. That is not true. The EU currently provides subsidies which result in the large-scale transport of animals. Does the Commission intend to take action to put an end to such subsidies?

 
  
 

The remarks attributed to the Member of the Commission concern the treatment of animals in a Community abattoir. No export refunds were payable in respect of these animals.

Animal welfare is an increasing social concern. Animal transport is the area that probably receives the widest public attention.

The Community has a key role to play in improving the conditions in which animals are reared, transported and slaughtered. The necessity to limit the transport of the animals over long distances only when it is really necessary, and to keep any suffering to a minimum, is an especially important objective.

The Commission has adopted in December 2000 a specific report on the implementation of existing Community legislation on the protection of animals during transport. The report concludes that Member States have clear difficulties in fully applying Community legislation on the protection of animals during transport. It must be pointed out that national authorities did not give the necessary priority to this area of Community legislation during past years.

Rules on animal protection during transport are there to be respected, just like any other law. The Commission will not tolerate the shortcomings in implementation found in the Report.

Animal welfare and, in particular, the protection of animals during transport is a priority for the Commission and it should be the same for Member States. In this framework the Commission is studying possible solutions to replace the transport of live animals by the transport of meat.

Where necessary, it will put forward proposals before the end of this year to improve the legislation's requirements with the view to facilitate proper enforcement. The Commission is working on short-term proposals to improve different technical aspects of the Community legislation and to facilitate cooperation and coordination between national authorities involved in international transport as well as in controls at national level.

The Commission will continue to actively check the enforcement of the Directive. Infringement proceedings have already been opened against some Member States in relation to their failure to implement Community legislation in this area. The Commission will be following future developments very closely and will quickly take appropriate follow up measures, including the opening of further infringement proceedings if no improvements are seen.

In relation to the payment of export refunds for live cattle from the Community, the honourable Member's attention is drawn to Commission Regulation (EC) No 615/98 which stipulates that the payment of export refunds is subject to compliance with Directive 91/628/EEC and provides for an appropriate monitoring system.

Furthermore, the Commission is currently carrying out an inquiry in several Member States with regard to the implementation of Regulation 615/98. The level of export aid is calculated with great care and limited to what is strictly necessary. It should be mentioned that, during the last year, subsidies paid for cattle traded for slaughtering were reduced significantly from 60.50 euros/100 kg to 41.00 euros/100 kg.

 

Question no 61 by Herman Schmid (H-0016/01)
 Subject: Non-aligned Member States
 

What is the Commission's view of the European Parliament's position that Member States which refrain from participating in EU military intervention should still be required to contribute to funding it? Does the Commission consider this to be compatible with non-alignment?

 
  
 

According to Article 28 of the Treaty on European Union the financing of military operations falls outside the scope of the Community budget and thereby also outside the scope of Community competence. The Commission is therefore not in a position to take a view on the important question put forward by the honourable Member. As far as is known, there is as yet no agreement in the Council on the method of financing of military operations undertaken by the Union.

 

Question no 62 by David Robert Bowe (H-0017/01)
 Subject: Reduction of mercury emissions from crematoria
 

As part of a strategy to reduce ‘background emissions’ of mercury into the environment, what role does the Commission believe measures to reduce emissions of mercury from crematoria could play in the strategy, and what scientific evidence does it have to justify its opinion?

 
  
 

Crematoria have been found to contribute about 1% to the total mercury emissions of the Community. With regard to this, crematoria are not considered to contribute substantially to air pollution by mercury and are not regulated by Community legislation on emission standards. Directive 96/62/EC on ambient air quality assessment and management (OJ L 296, 21.11.1996, p. 55) mentions mercury among those pollutants for which the setting of an ambient standard is to be considered. With regard to this the Commission is currently reviewing the role of anthropogenic mercury emissions in the total mercury cycle. In the course of this work the Commission will consider mercury emissions from crematoria in the context of the overall assessment of risk for human health and the environment.

 

Question no 63 by Fernando Pérez Royo (H-0020/01)
 Subject: Repair of the submarine 'HMS Tireless'
 

Has the Commission undertaken, or does it intend to undertake, any monitoring, supervisory or similar activity in respect of the repair of the submarine 'HMS Tireless' in the port of Gibraltar?

 
  
 

The honourable Member of Parliament wishes to know if the Commission has undertaken any monitoring, supervisory or similar activity in respect of the repair of the submarine 'HMS Tireless' in the port of Gibraltar.

The issue to which the honourable Member refers has also been the subject of several complaints to the Commission, most of which being very recent.

On the basis of the first complaint the Commission requested information from the British authorities by letter dated 10 October 2000 mainly concerning the existence of an intervention plan for the area of Gibraltar (Article 50 of Directive 96/29/Euratom), the measures taken to inform the public in the event of a radiological emergency relating to the repair operations of the submarine and the possible impact on Gibraltar and Spanish territory of the said operations.

The authorities of the United Kingdom answered by letters dated 14 November and 1 December 2000 that appropriate plans and information existed and the Commission has received a copy of the GIBPUBSAFE report. They furthermore confirmed that they are in regular contact with the Spanish authorities.

Although the reply can be considered to be satisfactory, it appears that further clarification is needed in order to complete the information already received by the Commission. In addition, new issues raised by recent complaints will be addressed to the Authorities of the United Kingdom very soon. Those include a request to the United Kingdom to give details of the current and planned radiological monitoring. On the basis of this reply, the Commission will be able to decide if there is justification for further actions.

 

Question no 64 by Jonas Sjöstedt (H-0024/01)
 Subject: Tax on financial transactions versus Article 56
 

Article 56 of the EC Treaty reads:

'1. Within the framework of the provisions set out in this Chapter, all restrictions on the movement of capital between Member States and between Member States and third countries shall be prohibited.

2. Within the framework of the provisions set out in this Chapter, all restrictions on payments between Member States and between Member States and third countries shall be prohibited.'

Does the Commission consider that a tax on financial transactions – 'Tobin tax' – is consistent with Article 56 of the EC Treaty?

 
  
 

The Commission is of the opinion:

that a tax of general application as originally conceived by Prof. Tobin (‘Tobin tax’), if introduced by a Member State, is likely to be contrary to Article 56, together with Articles 12, 39, 43, 49 and the corresponding articles of the EEA Agreement;

that such a tax, if introduced at Community level, is also unlikely to be possible under Article 57(2) of the EC Treaty, since that Article provides for restrictive measures only in a limited number of areas.

 

Question no 65 by Philip Charles Bradbourn (H-0025/01)
 Subject: Funding support for outside bodies
 

I refer to question H-0978/00(1) I asked at the plenary session in Strasbourg in January regarding the organisation PROMEURO and would ask the Commission to justify the expenditure of EUR 117 700 to an overtly propagandist organisation, and also to provide me with details, in writing if necessary, of any and all bodies and organisations with EU-wide individual memberships of less than 1 000 which the EU budget currently, or has in the last five years, supported either financially or in kind.

 
  
 

In the framework of a priority information campaign on the euro, part of the PRINCE programme and following a call for proposals aiming at the familiarisation with the euro as tomorrow’s common currency for 12 Member States, the Commission has selected those proposals which seemed to serve best that purpose.

Until now, the membership of a given organisation has never been a precondition for its participation to calls for proposals: no thresholds were ever set, and proposals have always been assessed on a case by case basis and in function of their intrinsic quality and interest, i.e. their capacity to reach the targeted audience and have the best impact within the limits of each individual proposal. Moreover, the efficiency of a given organisation has never depended on the importance of its membership.

To the extent the factor « membership » is traceable (*), the Commission is, of course, willing to provide the honourable Member with details of other bodies with a relatively small membership, but to trace them over the last five years will inevitably require time and personnel, so that in a first phase, the applications sent in 2000 would be screened, and only later could data covering the 1996 – 1999 period be provided.

(*) The term « membership » may be confusing: in certain cases, it can mean « affiliated organisations » (which is the case for large European professional Federations, often based in Brussels), in other cases « individual members » (which is not unusual for relatively small organisations within the civil society, or NGOs).

 
 

(1) Written answer of 16.1.2001.

 

Question no 66 by Mary Elizabeth Banotti (H-0029/01)
 Subject: Deep sea birds
 

What measures are currently being taken by the European Union to ensure that longline fishing ceases to be a hazard to great deep sea birds such as the Albatross given the decimation of this species by this form of fishing in recent years? If no such measures are being taken at present, what steps is the Commission prepared to take to ensure that such species are protected in the future?

 
  
 

To tackle this problem, the Community has already incorporated the following mitigation measures into legislation(1)1(2) 2: Using bird-scaring line with plastic streamers attached; Weighting the lines so that they sink faster and pose less risk; Prohibiting the discharge of offal at sea, which attracts seabirds to the lines; Setting the longlines at night, when albatrosses and other seabirds are less likely to be foraging. Using only thawed bait. These measures, which are compulsory for Community fishing companies, have been drawn up by the Council for the Conservation of Antarctic Marine Living Resources (CCAMLR), which deals with Antarctic waters, and of which the Community is a contracting party.

 
 

(1)1 Council Regulation (EC) No 66/98, OJ L 006, p. 1-17.
(2)2 Council Regulation (EC) No 2479/98, OJ L 309, p. 1-6.

 

Question no 67 by Carmen Cerdeira Morterero (H-0030/01)
 Subject: People with disabilities and maritime transport
 

Bearing in mind the Communication from the Commission COM(2000) 0284/final, the International Maritime Organisation (IMO) Recommendation on the design and operation of passenger ships to respond to elderly and disabled persons’ needs, the findings of the Handiami Project and Directive 1999/35/EC(1), does the Commission envisage taking steps or measures of any kind to avoid the situations that arise in maritime transport on a daily basis, whereby rights, safety and accessibility in this form of transport fail to meet the minimum levels required?

Is it aware that, on passenger ships, people with disabilities are obliged to spend the entire journey in the ship’s hold, designed to convey vehicles and merchandise, since many ferries and high-speed passenger ships do not provide people with disabilities with the means of gaining access to the passenger decks?

If so, what initiatives does it intend to take in this regard?

 
  
 

The Community has adopted over the last decade a number of measures relating to the safety of passenger vessels and of their passengers. The issue of disabled passengers has been specifically raised in the context of two of these instruments in terms which are in line with the International Maritime Organisation (IMO) recommendation referred to by the honourable Member

Council Directive 1999/35/EC(2)1 on the safe operation of regular ro-ro ferry and high speed passenger craft services contains an obligation for the operating companies "to ensure that general information about the services and assistance available to elderly and disabled persons on board is made known to the passengers and is made available in formats suitable for people with impaired sight". Furthermore, it requires Member States authorities to control documented evidence of safety training for personnel providing safety assistance to passengers, and in particular to elderly and disabled persons in an emergency.

Directive 1998/41/EC(3)2, making mandatory the registration of persons on board passenger ships, provides for the voluntary registration of information concerning the need for special care or assistance in emergency situations. Such information has to be communicated to the master before the departure of the ship.

Commenting on the facts reported by the honourable Member, the Commission wishes to underline that such practices go against the specific provisions of Directives 1998/18/EC(4) 3 and 1999/35/EC. Directive 1998/18/EC (introducing safety rules and standards for passenger ships) requires that the master, or the designated officer, shall ensure that, without the expressed consent of the master or the designated officer, no passengers are allowed access to an enclosed ro-ro deck when the ship is underway. Directive 1999/35/EC stipulates that the vehicle deck should be vacated by passengers before the ship sails and that they do not again have access until immediately prior to docking. On basis of the above, the Commission is, of course, ready to take appropriate action if formal complains were notified.

It is clear that while the above Directives deal with the safety of disabled people on board vessels, they do not address accessibility. However, following its recent Communication "Towards a barrier free Europe for people with disabilities", the Commission has undertaken the task to further examine issues relating to accessibility of disabled people to the means of transport.

 
 

(1) OJ L 138, 1.6.1999, p. 1
(2)1 OJ L 138, of 1.6.1999
(3)2 OJ L 188, of 2.7.1998
(4)3 OJ L 144, of 15.5.1998

 

Question no 68 by María Izquierdo Rojo (H-0033/01)
 Subject: Women and Taliban fundamentalism
 

What political measures is the European Union offering to women forced to endure the cruel Taliban regime? According to its information, how many women are subjected to this fundamentalist regime? Will the Commission appeal to Afghanistan’s neighbours to leave their borders open to Afghan refugee women? Will the European Union then grant financial support to those countries which admit refugee women?

 
  
 

The honourable Member is informed that on 22nd January 2001, the General Affairs Council agreed a revised version of the Union Common Position on Afghanistan.

Among other issues, this Common Position sets out the importance that the Union attaches ‘to respect for international Law and human rights, including the Convention on the elimination of all forms of discrimination against women’, denounces continuing discrimination against women in Afghanistan being ‘.. particularly concerned that this discrimination is hampering the humanitarian efforts of the international organisations and NGOs.’ The Common Position further notes in Article 1(c) that one of its objectives in Afghanistan is ‘to promote respect for international humanitarian law and human rights, including the rights of women and children’. Article 3(b) of the Common Position states that the Union ‘shall urge Afghan factions to end discriminatory policies and recognise, protect and promote the equal rights and dignity of men and women, including access to education and health facilities, employment, personal security and freedom from intimidation and harassment, and will emphasise the negative implications of discriminatory policies for the effective supply of aid’.

The honourable Member has asked for population figures, but she will appreciate that accurate figures with regard to populations inside Afghanistan are extremely difficult to come by. Estimates of the overall population used by international aid agencies and non governmental organisations (NGOs) working in Afghanistan vary from 20 to 25 million. If it is assumed that approximately half of this number is made up of women and girls and that approximately 90% live in Taliban controlled areas, then the number of women and girls in these areas would be between 9 and 11.25 million.

It should be noted that inside Afghanistan, NGOs implementing Community funded projects currently have access to all beneficiaries, women included, and can employ women as local NGO personnel in medical related programmes, in spite of the fact that separation between the two genders has to be strictly observed.

Taliban decrees prohibiting women from working have had differing effects depending upon the areas of activity and the geographical areas: Medical and food projects continue to employ women. After negotiations between the authorities and the concerned partners, Community funded Food for Work programmes have been able to restart after interruption.

With regard to appeals to neighbouring states, the Commission acts together with the Member States in matters concerning declarations, appeals or requests to third countries. On the occasion of the Union-Pakistan ad hoc political dialogue in Islamabad on 22/23 November 2000, the Union side urged Pakistan to reopen its borders to refugees from Afghanistan. More recently, on 17 January 2001, the Union issued a statement urging the Tajik authorities ‘to do everything in their power to alleviate the humanitarian situation of the refugees, in close cooperation with competent international bodies like UNHCR’.

Additionally, article 1(g) of the Common Position states that it is an objective of the Union ‘to support the programmes of international organisations and NGOs for Afghan refugee populations in neighbouring countries’. The honourable Member is informed that at the end of 2000, the Commission committed EUR 9 million in aid to uprooted people in Iran and Pakistan and that it is currently preparing a commitment to finance relief operations for refugees in Pakistan which will focus on the provision of drinking water and sanitation.

It can also be confirmed that the European Union is willing to consider assisting the Tajik authorities, both bilaterally and through ECHO, in the light of assessments and any subsequent appeals for assistance from international agencies.

 

Question no 69 by Robert J.E. Evans (H-0034/01)
 Subject: EC competition law
 

In the United Kingdom, independent retailers wishing to sell newspapers and other periodicals do not have a choice as to which distributor will deliver their newspapers from the depot. The supply business has been divided up into areas and each shop is allocated a distributing company, thereby creating a monopoly situation.

Many newsagents in London are dissatisfied with their supplier but at present are prohibited from changing to an alternative provider.

Does the Commission feel that this practice contravenes EC Competition rules?

 
  
 

The question put to the Commission concerns the application of the Community competition rules to the distribution of newspapers and periodicals.

First of all, it has to be noted that Community competition law is applicable if there is an appreciable effect on trade between Member States. According to the case-law of the Court of Justice, an agreement between undertakings or an abuse of a dominant position affects trade between Member States when it is capable of influencing, either directly or indirectly, actually or potentially, the pattern of trade between Member States.

The situation described in the question does not give indications as to the applicability of Community competition law, but seems to fall rather into the scope of application of the national competition rules.

It might, nevertheless, be useful to indicate that the Court of Justice has ruled in the Binon judgement(1)1 in 1985

- that a system of selective distribution may be established for the distribution of newspapers and periodicals, without infringing the prohibition contained in Article 85(1) [now Article 81(1)], given the special nature of those products as regards their distribution (they can only be sold by retailers during an extremely limited period of time, the publishers undertake to take back unsold copies),

- but that the permissibility of such a system depends in particular on the criteria governing the choice of distributors. Those criteria must be objective and of a qualitative nature. They must be laid down uniformly for all re-sellers and must not be applied in a discriminatory fashion.

 
 

(1)1 Judgement of the Court of 3 July 1985, Case 243/83, SA Binon & Cie v Sa Agence et messageries de la presse, [1985] ECR 2015.

 

Question no 70 by Liam Hyland (H-0035/01)
 Subject: Sheepmeat sector
 

Following the recent Communication from the Commission on the sheepmeat sector and a later conference in Brussels which was widely attended by representatives of the sheepmeat sector in the European Union, will the Commission outline what has subsequently happened in relation to these events and, in particular, from a monitoring perspective, will Commissioner Fischler outline what controls are in place to monitor lamb imports from New Zealand and other third countries to ensure that they fully respect EU guidelines, and will the Commissioner also indicate what steps he is taking to promote a sustainable wool sector?

 
  
 

Following the evaluation of the common market organisation for sheep and goat meat, the Commission is drawing up a proposal for a reform of the sector, which will be presented to the Council in the next few weeks.

With regard to imports, the Commission closely monitors the issue of import licences and, if necessary, where there is a risk of the quota being exceeded, informs the authorities in the Member States to suspend their issue. Given their sensitivity particular attention is paid to imports of chilled meat from New Zealand and their potential impact on the Community market. On the subject of wool, there are no measures that can be taken within the framework of the Common Agricultural Policy, as it is not classified as an agricultural product.

 

Question no 71 by Phillip Whitehead (H-0036/01)
 Subject: Radon gas
 

Does the Commission believe there is a danger to human health caused by exposure to naturally occurring radon gas? If so, what evidence has it to justify this concern? If not, why not?

 
  
 

Radon is a natural radioactive noble gas. Enhanced concentrations in air may be the cause of an increased risk of lung cancer. In particular, it is well known to have caused lung cancer amongst miners. The Commission has supported extensive research and numerous studies and conferences on this subject over the past decades.

Radon is dealt with in the following Community legislation: Council Directive 96/29/EURATOM of 13 May 1996(1)1 laying down basic safety standards for the protection of the health of workers and the general public against the dangers arising from ionising radiation, in particular with regard to the protection against radon in workplaces and Commission Recommendation 90/143/EURATOM of 21 February 1990(2)2 on the protection of the public against indoor exposure to radon.

The Commission keeps under review Member States implementation of this legislation and all other information on the issue.

 
 

(1)1 OJ L 159, 29.06.96
(2)2 OJ L 80, 27.03.90

 

Question no 72 by Patricia McKenna (H-0038/01)
 Subject: Food safety and farming methods
 

In view of the publication last month (16 January 2001) of proposals for a new Council Directive to improve the welfare of intensively kept pigs, and in the light of recent food safety concerns, brought into focus by BSE, will the Commission now encourage the use of more extensive and natural livestock farming systems which will pose less of a threat to food safety than intensive husbandry and which will also be responsible in terms of the environment and animal welfare?

Does the Commission agree that extensive systems of livestock farming are more acceptable to the majority of the EU public than intensive systems?

Could the Commission explain what specific measures it intends to take in order to encourage more extensive systems of farming?

 
  
 

The use of extensive farming methods for meat production in the Community and the encouragement thereof under the CAP vary considerably from one sector to another, depending on the structure and content of the respective COMs.

In the case of beef and veal, specific measures exist in the context of the premiums offered to producers with a view to encouraging them to shift to more extensive forms of farming.

The breeding premiums are limited on the basis of the fodder capacity of each holding in relation to the number and species of livestock qualifying for a premium. Granting of the premiums (special premium and suckler cow premium) is subject to compliance with a maximum density factor of two adult bovine units (ABUs) per hectare and per calendar year.

In addition, in order to consolidate incentives to the extensification of production with a view to improving their effectiveness vis-à-vis the environmental objectives, a supplementary amount is granted to producers benefiting from the special premium and/or the suckler cow premium who comply with certain genuine, rigorous, requirements concerning the density factor.

Agenda 2000 has greatly reinforced this measure by almost trebling the amount of aid and limiting the areas to be considered to open fields only, while also taking account of all the livestock present on a farm.

This extensification payment is, in principle, EUR 100 per special premium and suckler cow premium granted, provided the density factor of the holding is equal to or less than 1.4 ABU per hectare for the calendar year concerned. Member States may decide to phase this measure in, choosing from a number of differentiated arrangements.

In the case of sheepmeat, it is well known that this form of production relies almost exclusively on extensive farming methods. The measures applied under the COM in sheepmeat accordingly support this form of production.

The COM in pigmeat does not include any support instrument addressed directly to pigmeat producers. It is therefore difficult to influence their choice of farming methods under the CAP. However, in response to consumer and retailer demand, certain producers have, in the last few years, converted to extensive methods. It is very possible that more producers will be encouraged to follow this example by the current debate on food safety and animal welfare.

Finally, the Commission draws the honourable Member's attention to the linkage established between direct support payments to producers and environmental requirements with the Agenda 2000 CAP reform. Member States are now required to take the measures which they consider appropriate in view of the situation of the agricultural land use or the production concerned and which reflect the potential environmental effects. Member States have a number of options insofar as the measures are concerned, including agri-environment commitments, respect for general environmental requirements and specific environmental requirements constituting a condition for direct payments. This measure, the details of the application of which are currently being discussed, has great potential, if well implemented by Member States, to contribute to environmental improvement and sustainable development in agriculture.

 

Question no 73 by Paul Rübig (H-0039/01)
 Subject: EU rules on the mutual recognition of fishing permits
 

In most EU Member States there are official or semi-official types of fishing permits enabling the holder to fish in the Member State or Land in question (for example in Austria, Germany or South Tyrol).

The Land of Upper Austria moved towards liberalisation a decade ago with its Fishing Law which recognised fishing permits (provided they are valid and contain a passport photo) from other Austrian Länder and from other countries, too, as having equal validity.

This means that an angler from another Land or another country who has a permit issued by that Land or country only needs to obtain a local fishing permit, but does not need to buy a visitor's permit. Unfortunately, this is possible in only a few Länder in Austria and Germany.

Would it not therefore be appropriate to introduce an EU fishing permit, in other words a permit valid in all Member States?

 
  
 

The Community has exclusive jurisdiction over the conservation and management of fisheries resources. This jurisdiction applies to waters that are under national jurisdiction (including internal waters) and to the high seas (see the first article of Council Regulation (EEC) No 3760/92 of 20 December 1992 establishing a Community system for fisheries and aquaculture). At present, the Community has not yet adopted any Community Regulation for the mutual recognition of angling permits, nor does it as yet have any plans to do so, unless such a request were to be formulated by a group of Member States.

 

Question no 74 by Alain Lipietz (H-0047/01)
 Subject: Sustainable long-term strategy in Gothenburg
 

Will a proposal for a long-term strategy dovetailing policies for economically, socially and ecologically sustainable development be presented to the Gothenburg Summit, as required by the Helsinki conclusions, and what will it contain?

 
  
 

Yes, the Commission will submit an European Union sustainable development strategy to the Gothenburg European Council, in line with the task received at the Helsinki European Council. The strategy, which will focus on European Sustainable Development questions, as distinct from global ones, the latter being at the centre of the Rio+10 conference in 2002, will attempt to give a concrete operational content to sustainable development. It will do so by concentrating on what the Commission considers the main areas where developments threaten to become unsustainable, including social exclusion (poverty, ageing of the European society, some disquieting public health trends, climate change and clean energy, mobility and land use, the management of natural resources).

It will propose conclusions for the Gothenburg European Council that will put sustainable development firmly on the European agenda with regular reviews at the Spring European Councils of progress made.

 

Question no 75 by Gérard Onesta (H-0048/01)
 Subject: Integration and SDS
 

How will the Commission's proposal for a long-term strategy dovetailing policies for economically, socially and ecologically sustainable development link to the so-called 'Cardiff process' of environmental integration and to the Sixth Environmental Action Programme?

 
  
 

The European Union sustainable development strategy, the Cardiff process and the 6th Environmental Action Programme are closely related but are nonetheless quite distinct. The strategy is much wider as it also encompasses social and economic dimensions and as it takes a clearly longer term view, that of one generation ahead. Cardiff pursues a key instrument of sustainable development, namely the integration of environmental concerns into the decisions of other sectoral Council formations, but a successful strategy needs to rely on a wider array of tools, such as market reform and an active technology policy. Many of the themes in the 6th Environmental Action Programme will come back in the European Union strategy, though by no means all. Not all environmental problems should be treated within the context of sustainable development. For that they have to entail severe socio-economic consequences and tend to be irreversible.

 

Question no 76 by Mark Francis Watts (H-0049/01)
 Subject: Role of Parliament in SDS
 

How will the European Parliament be consulted about the proposal for a long-term strategy dovetailing polices for economically, socially and ecologically sustainable development required by the Helsinki conclusions?

 
  
 

The Commission will issue a working document at the end of March for a discussion with the other European institutions and civil society on an European Union sustainable development strategy. The President of the Commission has written a letter to Parliament President Fontaine suggesting the organisation by Parliament, with the help of the Commission, of a major hearing on sustainable development in the second half of April. In May, the Commission will adopt its strategy proposal, several weeks in advance of the Gothenburg Summit.

 

Question no 77 by Nuala Ahern (H-0051/01)
 Subject: Safety of High-Level Radioactive Wastes at Sellafield
 

In its report, issued in December 2000, on the safety status of the Highly-Active Storage Tanks (HASTs) for High-Level Radioactive Wastes (HLW) at Sellafield, the Radiological Protection Institute of Ireland (RPII) identified a series of outstanding concerns including:

- the need for improved data on the reliability of components whose performance impinges on safety;

- the fact that the risk of damage from a severe earthquake has not been fully analysed;

- the fact that there is currently no instrumentation for detecting possible hydrogen build-up in the HASTs;

- the lack of a programme for random checking of personnel for alcohol/drug abuse; and

- the fact that the consequences of a very severe accident have not been adequately assessed.

Has the Commission examined this report produced by one of the safety authorities of a neighbouring state to the UK? What steps does it intend to take to press BNFL and the UK Government to improve the safety of the storage of existing nuclear materials, including HLW, at Sellafield?

 
  
 

The Commission thanks the honourable Member for drawing its attention to the report by the Radiological Protection Institute of Ireland (RPII) on the tanks for the storage of highly active waste.

The Commission has a copy of the report in question. The specific points raised in the honourable Member’s question should be considered in the context of the full report text.

The responsibility for regulating the Sellafield plant rests with the United Kingdom's Nuclear Installations Inspectorate (NII), which produced its own report on these tanks earlier in 2000. The RPII findings and recommendations broadly concur with those of the British regulator. According to a recent press release by BNFL (dated 4/12/00) on the subject of the RPII report, all of the recommendations made in the report have already been implemented or are part of their continuing dialogue with the NII.

Question no 78 by Neil MacCormick (H-0053/01)
 Subject: Insurance in the single market
 

What plans has the Commission to review the Third Life and Non-Life Insurance Directives with a view to enabling companies to offer insurance services to the entire single market? How will it seek to overcome the distorting effects for companies of having to comply with the national marketing/conduct of business rules of each Member State in which they wish to sell insurance.

 
  
 

The insurance directives have completed the creation of the internal market in insurance. They institute a regime of approval and prudential supervision exercised once by the insurer's Member State of origin ('European passport'), which enables an insurer to operate in the Community on the basis of freedom of establishment or to provide services, subject to a single financial control regime.

The implementation of the directives has already had significant effects: insurers' international activities have increased, and competition on national markets has been boosted, in terms both of prices and of new products offered on the national markets. However, the impact of the internal market has been less pronounced, especially in the case of crossborder insurance transactions, for both the retail and wholesale markets (involving major industrial and commercial risks) which represent some 50% of the market.

The action plan for financial services endorsed by the Cologne European Council in 1999 lays down a programme of actions and priorities with a view to obtaining maximum advantage from the internal market in financial services, especially as regards the retail or consumer markets. Specific actions are proposed for insurance. In this regard, reference should be made to the proposal for a directive on insurance mediation submitted by the Commission in September 2000, the interpretative communication on the freedom to provide services of general interest in the insurance sector (February 2000), the proposal for a directive on distance selling in financial services, and the work in progress on e-commerce and financial services.

 

Question no 79 by Juan de Dios Izquierdo Collado (H-0056/01)
 Subject: Widespread fraud in the fibre flax sector
 

The Special Unit for Financial Crime and Corruption of the Public Prosecutor’s Office in Spain has drawn up a report on this matter, concluding that: ‘from the investigations carried out by the Special Unit, it is clear that there is widespread fraud in the application of the Community aid system for the fibre flax sector in Spain, amounting to much more than mere irregularities’.

Senior officials at the Ministry of Agriculture, who were named but not dismissed after the fraud allegations by Vice-President Loyola de Palacio became known, were directly involved both in drawing up the national rules governing this aid and in arrangements for processing the product, since they or members of their immediate family own processing plants.

In the light of the undertakings given by the Commission President on this matter, what are President Prodi’s views on the Special Unit’s report, which OLAF also helped to compile, the measures taken by Mrs Loyola de Palacio at the time and the statements she has made to the European Parliament? Given the information available to him, what steps will the Commission President take to meet his responsibilities in this area? When will the final OLAF report be available?

 
  
 

The Commission has no desire to declare an opinion on a report drawn up by a national Public Prosecutor’s Office, particularly before the competent authorities in the Member State in question have had an opportunity to determine whether it is appropriate to draw conclusions, pursuant to national law, from such a report.

The Commission will, if necessary, declare its opinion on an OLAF report when this becomes available. At present, the Commission is not in a position to announce when the relevant OLAF report will be available.

In his speech to the Conference of Presidents of 7 September 1999, the Commission President stated, “I reiterate my willingness to reinforce our cooperation and my confidence in your Assembly.” In line with this policy, the Commission is taking action and will, in future, take action and will provide Parliament, if necessary, with all the information available to it on the matter in question.

On the basis of the documents currently available to the Commission, the Commission President can only reiterate his complete confidence in Loyola de Palacio.

nswer

Question no 88 by Yasmine Boudjenah (H-0078/01)
 Subject: Situation in Western Sahara
 

In crossing Western Sahara without the prior authorisation of the Western Saharan government, the organisers of the Paris-Dakar rally risked in an irresponsible and criminal way contributing towards bringing about a resumption of the fighting in a conflict which has still to be settled after 25 years. The event gave backing to the Moroccan Government which claims sovereignty over Western Sahara in defiance of international law. The cease-fire which has held since 1991 is now fragile. The situation could flare up at any moment and degenerate into war, destroying all the efforts made to achieve peace and harming the country's future for a long time to come.

Urgent action is therefore necessary in order to avoid any armed reopening of hostilities, to hold the referendum which has been promised, but continually postponed, since January 1992 and, finally, to achieve compliance by Morocco with the United Nations peace plan.

For the sake of the right of peoples to self-determination, and in order effectively and swiftly to help avoid an irreparable situation, is the Commission resolved to play an active role to that end, and in what form?

 
 

Question no 89 by Margot Keßler (H-0089/01)
 Subject: Western Sahara
 

This year's Paris-Dakar Rally once again passed through territory occupied in breach of international law. In so doing, a driver was seriously injured by a landmine, aggravating the already tense situation in that region. The Polisario liberation movement put its soldiers on a state of alert after Moroccan army units had crossed the embankment along a five-kilometre section where the rally went through. What action does the Commission intend to take in order to counter the acute danger of war, maintain the cease-fire and contribute to a lasting peace settlement?

Joint Answer

The Commission is following closely the progress being made in implementing the plan for settling the Western Sahara dispute, adopted by the United Nations Security Council. Like the Member States, the Commission fully supports the efforts of the United Nations Secretary-General and his Personal Envoy, Mr James Baker, to find a fair, lasting, and sustainable solution for Western Sahara. The Union is in close contact with Mr Baker to whom it has been expressed readiness to assist in concrete terms the different stages of the implementation of the plan of the United Nations, from the identification of voters to the repatriation of refugees and the rehabilitation of the territory.

At the occasion of his recent visit to the Maghreb countries, the President of the Commission stressed the importance of strengthened dialogue within the Arab Maghreb Union. It is hoped that direct talks between the interest parties in an open dialogue will lead out of the current deadlock.

As to the fisheries agreement with Morocco, the existing agreement has expired and the Commission is in the process of negotiating a new agreement. In the expired agreement, as in the ongoing negotiations, the Commission has been referring to the fishing zone of Morocco being defined by the "water falling within sovereignty and/or within the jurisdiction of Morocco". This definition does not prejudge in any way the statute of the waters in the Saharan fishing bank. The latter is governed by international law, and in particular the various United Nations' resolutions, to which the Union constantly brought its support.

 
 

Question no 90 by John Joseph McCartin (H-0061/01)
 Subject: BSE crisis
 

Can the Commission state which Member States of the EU have been involved in the destruction of beef following the recent BSE crisis and does the Commission consider that this destruction of beef, as a market measure when the beef has tested negative to BSE, is an acceptable public policy and does the Commission consider that as an alternative policy the canning of the product for use as emergency food aid is feasible?

 
  
 

The following Member States have notified the Commission that the purchase for destruction scheme applicable for bovine animals above 30 months is up and running: Belgium, Spain, France, Ireland, Luxembourg and Portugal. Germany and Italy are in the process of preparing the implementation of the Commission Regulation concerned (2777/2000).

Germany and Luxembourg are the only two Member States so far authorised to apply Article 3(3) of that Regulation which allows for the possibility to destroy also animals above 30 months having tested negative.

That measure is considered to be the most cost efficient market measure available under the present circumstances. Canning of beef for use as emergency food aid is, in theory, an alternative outlet but in practice the experience with such an operation has been extremely negative for a number of reasons e.g. costs, control, logistics, local acceptance, etc.

 

Question no 91 by Olivier Dupuis (H-0070/01)
 Subject: Financing of the University of Tetovo
 

On 5 October 2000, in reply to a question from the European Parliament on the subject, Commissioner Verheugen stated that the Commission intended to make available EUR 1 million for the current year to support the implementation of the law on higher education in Macedonia.

During the meeting of the EU/Macedonia joint parliamentary delegation held this week at the European Parliament, three months after Commissioner Verheugen’s statement, a Commission representative confirmed that the sum of EUR 1 million would be made available, together with further allocations amounting to EUR 5 million. Asked when the payments would actually be made, the Commission representative remained vague, saying it would be sometime in the spring. Is the Commission aware that any further delay in paying out the funds concerned is likely to jeopardise the implementation of the project as a whole? Is the Commission aware of the importance of this project, particularly as regards conflict prevention policy, which is one of its priorities? Finally, given the overall cost of the project (in the region of 22 million dollars) and its importance, does the Commission not think it could increase its total contribution?

 
  
 

The Commission supports the establishment of the South East Europe University (SEEU) in FYROM as an important step in providing for the educational rights of the Albanian minority there.

The Commission is prepared to propose a total contribution of EUR 5 million for the SEEU. A sum of EUR 1 million has already been committed under the 2000 budget through a Commission Decision of 17 November. The procedures are now underway to facilitate payment of this amount to the University by May. The Commission will propose that a further EUR 4 million be committed under the 2001 budget. These funds should be transferred to the University before the second half of this year.

The Community commitment to the SEEU is seen as a necessary guarantee for smaller donors contemplating participation in the project and an important factor contributing towards a successful outcome. The Commission understands that the total amount which has been pledged so far does not fully cover the needs. However, the Organisation for Security and Cooperation in Europe (OSCE) has not signalled that this would hinder the initiative from getting off the ground.

The Commission is supporting the initiative precisely for the reasons of its significance in terms of ethnic reconciliation between the populations in the country.

The Commission is not in a position to fully finance the SEEU. This would imply using up the bulk of funding available for FYROM for this project. In addition to having to address other needs, not least the implementation of obligations under the Stabilisation and Association Agreement, such an unbalanced allocation of funds would not be acceptable to the national authorities: it could even heighten ethnic tensions rather then soothe them.

It is important to refer from now on to the South East Europe (SEE) University rather than to the Tetovo University since the latter term refers to the existing unrecognised University. Given the sensitivity of the issue, we should not burden this new initiative with the heritage of that University.

The Commission will keep the honourable Member informed about any further developments.

 

Question no 93 by Anders Wijkman (H-0073/01)
 Subject: Commission activities with regard to Export Credit Agencies
 

Export Credit Agencies (ECAs) play a very important role in support of infrastructure projects in the South and in Eastern Europe. During the 1990’s, ECAs’ financing through loans, project guarantees and investment insurance averaged USD 80-100 billion per annum. Roughly 60% was channelled in support of energy-intensive projects: fossil fuel power plants, oil and gas developments, transportation, etc. While a few – most notably the US Export-Import Bank – weight environmental impact in deciding whether to make loans, most do not. This means that taxpayers money is being used to subsidise investments in carbon-based technologies. Given the seriousness of the climate problem – and EU commitments to the Kyoto Protocol – it is remarkable that so little attention has been paid to the critical role played by ECAs.

What steps will the Commission take to work towards the introduction and harmonisation of strict environmental guidelines for European ECA activities?

 
  
 

No specific environmental export credit agencies (ECA) guidelines are foreseen for ECA financing or insurance support in relation to power plants functioning with carbon-based technologies.

However, at this moment, a draft Agreement on Common Approaches on Environment and Officially Supported Export Credits is being discussed among the Members of the Organisation for Economic Cooperation and Development’s (OECD) Working Party on Export Credits and Credit Guarantees.

In this draft common approaches are being elaborated for identifying, evaluating and taking decisions on the environmental impact of projects and goods and/or services benefiting from officially supported export credits.

For this work a mandate was given at the level of the OECD Council of Ministers in order to strengthen common approaches on environment and Officially Supported Export Credits by the end of 2001. This mandate was renewed in 2000 when OECD Ministers welcomed the progress towards common approaches.

G-8 Heads of State, at the occasion of the summit in Cologne in 1999, also confirmed the commitment to work within the OECD towards common environmental guidelines for Export Credit Agencies.

The deadline for this work program is the end of 2001 with intermediary reporting at the occasion of the coming OECD Meeting at Ministerial level (14-17 May 2001).

 

Question no 94 by Bernd Posselt (H-0080/01)
 Subject: Traditional means of preparing foodstuffs in the Czech Republic
 

There are reports in the Czech media that following the Czech Republic's accession to the EU the extremely popular sausage speciality 'Utopenec' (pickled in a jar with vinegar, paprika and onions and gradually eaten and kept sealed in the jar) and the traditional process of distilling Moravian slivovitz would have to be banned. Is this true, and is the Commission aware that this could incite anti-EU propaganda?

 
  
 

The Community acquis on food safety contains detailed provisions on the safety requirements (hygiene, additives, flavourings, etc.) and on labelling of foodstuffs. Any new Member State has to be in a position to apply fully the Community acquis by the date of accession. This is why all candidate countries are encouraged to apply the food safety legislation well before accession so as to offer the possibility to both the administrative structures and the food operators to adapt gradually to the Community system. This is to ensure that the internal market functions properly from the date of accession and that consumers are protected and correctly informed throughout the enlarged Community.

The Community acquis on food also includes a quality policy, i.e. provisions on specific products, for which there is a possibility of seeking protection for geographical names and traditional methods against misuse, misleading use and imitation. If candidate countries wish to seek such protection by accession, they have to make a formal request to the Commission, which will be examined in detail.

The Commission is not aware of any request for derogation of the product Utopenec from the Community acquis and does not know whether the Czech Republic intends to seek protection of this product under the Community rules on quality policy.

As regards slivovitz, Council Regulation (EEC) N° 1576/89 lays down general rules on the definition, description and presentation of spirit drinks. At present the production method of slivovitz appears not to comply with the requirements laid down by this Regulation for the category “fruit spirits”. If slivovitz does not comply with the acquis requirements on fruit spirits upon accession, it may not bear the name fruit spirit, unless otherwise agreed within the framework of the accession negotiations.

 

Question no 95 by Avril Doyle (H-0084/01)
 Subject: Cattle purchase for destruction scheme
 

Did the Commission envisage the mass slaughter of young untested cattle under 30 months in the ‘purchase for destruction scheme’?

If the ‘purchase for destruction scheme’ is a public health/consumer confidence issue, how can the Commission justify the fact that Ireland has destroyed over 30 000 young and healthy cattle under 30 months and virtually no older animals (as of 24 January 2001), as the balance of the price structure favours steers and heifers and is a serious disincentive to the destruction of the older cull cows, where the residue of the BSE infection is most likely to be?

 
  
 

It is not correct that animals younger than 30 months can be purchased for destruction under Regulation (EC) 2777/2000 and the Commission has absolutely no information that Ireland is destroying cattle under 30 months.

 

Question no 96 by Antonios Trakatellis (H-0087/01)
 Subject: Deletion of references to nationality from identity papers and illegal naturalisation in Greece
 

The findings of the committee of inquiry into the Greek general elections (9 April 2000) revealed that thousands of people who were not entitled to Greek nationality had become illegally naturalised and that a large number of irregularities had occurred in this connection. Five thousand cases of such illegal activity have already been discovered at the Moscow Consulate involving officials working for the diplomatic service. The Ministry of Foreign Affairs ordered an official inquiry into the ‘Greek naturalisation industry’, which had been in operation since 1993. Despite the fact that the acquisition and loss of nationality is the sole responsibility of the Member States, in view of the fact that the Greek Government is seeking the deletion of references to nationality from identity papers contrary to Directives 68/360/EEC(1) and 73/148/EEC(2) which specifically require the nationality to be indicated.

Can such measures be regarded as being in accordance with Community legislation and what measures will the Commission take in response to infringements thereof, given that they will make it impossible to establish nationality and hence to monitor the illegal entry and movement of third-country nationals within the territory of the Member States? What action will be taken by the European Union in response to the illegal granting of citizenship of Member States and, by extension, of Europe, and what action can be taken to put an end to this state of affairs? Has information been gathered with regard to naturalisation processes in Greece and other Member States and should the approximation of legislation regarding the acquisition of nationality in the EU Member States be considered?

 
  
 

There are two parts to the honourable Member’s question, the first relating to the failure to specify nationality in the new Greek identity papers and the second to possible irregularities committed in attributing Greek nationality to certain persons.

As regards the first aspect, the Commission must inform you that it has, in a letter of 4 December 2000, already drawn the attention of the Greek Government to the fact that the deletion of references to nationality in Greek identity papers may be in contravention of Community law, particularly of the two directives to which the honourable Member refers, and may constitute an obstacle to the free movement of Greek citizens on Member State territory. Article 2(2) of Council Directives 68/360/EEC, of 15 October 1968, on the abolition of restrictions on movement and residence within the Community for workers of Member States and their families, and 73/148/EEC, of 21 May 1973, on the abolition of restrictions on movement and residence within the Community for nationals of Member States with regard to the establishment and provision of services, stipulate that “Member States shall, acting in accordance with their laws, issue to their nationals, or renew, an identity card or passport, which shall state in particular the holder's nationality.” This is also applicable to other classes of citizen within the European Union, particularly to citizens other than workers, insofar as Directives 90/364, 90/365 and 93/96 refer to the aforementioned directives.

The Greek Government replied in a letter of 18 January 2001. Their answer specified that Greek identity papers have Hellenic Republic in the header and are, moreover, issued exclusively to Greek nationals. The holder of Greek identity papers must necessarily, therefore, have Greek nationality without the need to specify this. The Greek Government acknowledged, nonetheless, that the matter of the deletion of references to nationality in the new Greek identity papers is currently being examined in the greatest detail in anticipation of a ruling pending in a case before the Supreme Administrative Court in Greece.

The Commission is thus unable to declare an opinion until the Greek Authorities have adopted a definitive position.

On the second aspect, it should be noted that, as the honourable Member points out, the granting of nationality of a Member State is a matter of national jurisdiction (see Declaration 2 on nationality of a Member State, annexed to the Final Act of the Conference of the Representatives of the Governments of the Member States establishing the Treaty on European Union, OJ 1992, C 191, page 98). Nonetheless, exercise of this jurisdiction must comply with Community law (see: Judgement of the Court of Justice of 7 July 1992 in case C-369/90, Micheletti, European Court Reports 1992 page I-4239, item 10: “Under international law, it is for each Member State, having due regard to Community law, to lay down the conditions for the acquisition and loss of nationality.”)

In view of the above, the European Union is not authorised to proceed to harmonise national legislative texts on the granting of nationality. Furthermore, the European Commission may not act in place of the competent national administrative and judicial authorities and declare an opinion on any possible irregularities committed in the granting of Greek nationality to certain persons.

Finally, it should be pointed out that persons holding valid Greek identity papers or passports will have no problem in travelling and residing freely within the territory of EU Member States. For reasons of legal security, it is commonly accepted that the holder of a valid travel document may exercise all the rights arising from possession of such a document until it is cancelled, withdrawn or until it expires.

 
 

(1) OJ L 257, 19.10.1968, p. 13
(2) OJ L 172, 28.6.1973, p. 14

Despite the reassuring answer given by the Commission to my previous question (H-0989/00)(1) about when Spata airport would start operating and road access to this airport, public concern in Greece about this matter has been increasing.
