(1) Written answer of 16.1.2001

 

Question no 98 by Inglewood (H-0091/01)
 Subject: White Paper on Environmental Liability
 

Will the European Commission publish a full economic analysis of its proposals involving environmental liability to be set out in its forthcoming legislative proposals?

 
  
 

The Commission is in the process of carrying out a full economic analysis of environmental liability. Following the publication in 1993 of its Green Paper on Remedying Environmental Damage and in preparation for its White Paper on Environmental Liability, the Commission in 1996 published a study entitled "Economic Aspects of Liability and Joint Compensation Systems for Remedying Environmental Damage". Fulfilling the commitment it made in the White Paper to study in more depth the economic issues involved in liability, the Commission launched seven new studies: four on financial insurance issues, one focused on industry competitiveness issues, one on the impact of the proposals on prevention of damage and one on the valuation and restoration of biodiversity damage. In addition, the Commission has also commissioned an update of the legal study which was carried out in 1996. The results of all of these new studies will be published on the Commission WebPages dedicated to environmental liability(1)1. The results of two of the studies on the financial assurance issues and of the study on competitiveness are already available on those WebPages.

The forthcoming legislative proposal will take into account the results of this economic analysis.

 
 

(1)1 Http://www.europa.eu.int/comm/environment/liability/followup.htm.

 

Question no 99 by Pat the Cope Gallagher (H-0093/01)
 Subject: European Anglers' Alliance and EU funding for study on the benefits of recreational angling
 

The European Anglers’ Alliance is a pan-European organisation with 6 million members in 20 countries. Will the Commission state its position on the role of recreational angling in the European Union, and will it give an undertaking to fund the EAA’s study on the ‘socio-economic benefits of recreational angling in the European Union’, the aim of which is to incorporate recreational angling into legislation on fisheries?

 
  
 

There is no doubt that recreational angling is a widespread spare time activity all over Europe: in certain areas it is also an important factor of the local economy, mainly of the tourist industry. In some cases both recreational anglers and professional fishermen exploit the same resource, which leads to a certain competition between the two.

The European Angler’s Alliance (EAA) has contacted the Commission and a meeting was held in November 2000. It was agreed that the EAA would be informed about the Work Programme of the Advisory Committee on Fisheries and Aquaculture, in order to allow closer involvement of recreational anglers in discussion within that Committee of matters of special interest to them, or on a case by case basis.

As far as the proposed study mentioned in the honourable Member’s question is concerned, the Commission is unable to support any additional studies in the current planning period due to priorities imposed by the need for research on other aspects of fisheries management and also due to budgetary constraints.

 

Question no 101 by Karin Riis-Jørgensen (H-0096/01)
 Subject: Price freeze on medicines in Denmark
 

In November 2000, the Danish Government introduced a price freeze and price cap on medicines, requiring medicine prices to be reduced to the European average. Companies selling medicines solely in Denmark, however, are not required to reduce their prices.

Does the Commission take the view that the introduction of a price freeze on medicines in Denmark is consistent with internal market rules and, in particular, with Article 28 of the Treaty?

What representations will the Commission make to the Danish Government and can the Commission envisage the Danish Government paying compensation to the companies concerned for this unnecessary distortion of competition on the Danish market?

 
  
 

The Commission has received complaints on this issue and is currently examining the Danish measure in the light of the principle set forth in Articles 28 to 30 (ex Articles 30-36) of the EC Treaty, prohibiting unjustified trade barriers, and the jurisprudence of the Court of Justice. The Court has stated that a national price regulation could be contrary to the principle of free movement of goods when certain conditions are fulfilled. At this stage the Commission is awaiting supplementary information to be supplied by the complainants illustrating the effects of the Danish measure before taking any further steps. When the Commission has examined this information in the light of Articles 28 to 30 of the EC Treaty it will decide on whether the Danish measure constitutes an infringement of Community law.

With regard to the question of remedies, the Court of Justice has pronounced itself in its jurisprudence (joined cases C-46/93 and C-48/93 “Brasserie du Pêcheur”). It has stated that in the event of a breach of Community law, the principle whereby a State is liable for loss and damage caused to individuals is inherent in the system of the Treaty. Therefore, when a breach of Community law by a Member State is attributable to the national legislature acting in a field in which it has a wide discretion to make legislative choices, individuals suffering loss or injury thereby are entitled to reparation where the rule of Community law breached is intended to confer rights upon them, the breach is sufficiently serious and there is a direct causal link between the breach and the damage sustained by the individuals.

 

Question no 102 by Ioannis Marinos (H-0097/01)
 Subject: Honouring by Turkey of its obligations vis-à-vis the EU
 

In the Customs Union Agreement concluded in 1995 with the European Union, Turkey undertook (Article 16, paragraph 1) to conclude a free trade agreement with Cyprus by 31 December 2000. The text of the EU-Turkey partnership agreement, as agreed at political level by the General Affairs Council on 4 December 2000, contains a conditionality clause which covers compliance by Turkey with its obligations under the association agreement and the Customs Union.

Will the Commission say whether Turkey has fulfilled the obligation it assumed relating to the free trade agreement with the Republic of Cyprus? Does the use of the conditionality clause referred to above require a proposal by the Commission to the Council which decides by qualified majority? If so, does the Commission intend to make a proposal to this effect to the Council of Ministers of the EU?

 
  
 

The General Affairs Council reached a political decision on 4 December 2000 on the regulation establishing the legal basis of the Accession Partnership with Turkey and on a single framework for coordinating Community financial resources for Turkey.

As indicated in the Regular Report 2000 on the progress of Turkey towards accession, Turkey has not yet concluded agreements with a certain number of countries linked to the Community by trade agreements, as required under the Union-Turkey Customs Union.

Concerning Cyprus, Turkey has not yet started negotiations for a Free Trade Agreement. This issue has been raised with the Turkish authorities in the relevant sub-committees of the Association Committee and in the Customs Union Joint Committee.

Following approval of the above-mentioned regulation, the Commission would have the responsibility of deciding whether a proposal is needed for taking steps in the event of non-fulfilment of Turkey’s obligations. Such a proposal would require approval by a qualified majority in the Council.

 

Question no 103 by Struan Stevenson (H-0098/01)
 Subject: Cat and dog fur

The honourable Member is asked to refer to the Commission's answer to his written question E-3981/001(1).

On the question of health risks, the preservation treatments used when tanning skins and pelts make them inert and therefore do not convert them into transmission agents for animal diseases. In this connection, no evidence exists of any public health risk, given that the objects concerned are not considered as equivalent to living matter.

Question no 104 by Paulo Casaca (H-0099/01)
 Subject: Implementation of the new Article 100 of the Treaty of Nice
 

The Treaty of Nice has amended Article 100 TEC to read as follows: '2. Where a Member State is in difficulties or is seriously threatened with severe difficulties caused by natural disasters or exceptional occurrences beyond its control, the Council, acting by a qualified majority on a proposal from the Commission, may grant, under certain conditions, Community financial assistance to the Member State concerned.'

Bearing in mind the severe damage caused by heavy flooding in large areas of the centre and north of continental Portugal and in view of the practice adopted in respect of certain clauses of the Treaty of Amsterdam which were implemented even before ratification, does the Commission intend to propose to the Council that emergency aid should be allocated to Portugal to repair the damage caused by this disaster?

 
  
 

The Commission is aware of the extent of the disaster which has struck Portugal and the extent of the damage inflicted. It offers its condolences to the families of the victims and its deepest sympathy to all those persons affected by these terrible floods.

The Commission has several times had occasion to outline the resources available to it in order to contribute towards offsetting the effects of natural disasters by means of existing financial instruments. There is indeed some margin for action within the Structural Fund programmes. As the Commissioner responsible for regional policy announced in the plenary sitting in Brussels on 31 January 2001, the Commission proposes, in close liaison with the Portuguese Government and the local authorities, to look into the possibilities for reallocating resources from the Structural Fund programmes which have already been approved for the period 2000-2006 in order to assist the affected areas.

The Commission would point out that the budget line for the allocation of emergency aid to victims of disasters within the Community ceased to exist in 1998. The question of reintroducing a specific budget line was raised in the course of the budgetary procedure for 2001 but was not retained in the budget adopted. At the present time, therefore, there is no budget framework for Community emergency aid to the flood victims in Portugal.

The Treaty of Nice redefines Article 100 TEC and specifies that the decision is taken by qualified majority. The article does not specify conditions for the granting of Community financial assistance. Nonetheless, a Declaration which will be appended to the Treaty of Nice stipulates that such assistance must comply with the financial perspectives.

 

Question no 105 by Olle Schmidt (H-0100/01)
 Subject: EU customs duties and agricultural policy
 

During the 1990s, the economic situation for people in the world's least developed countries scarcely improved. High customs duties and other barriers to trade were amongst the reasons for this. The UN has calculated that developing countries as a whole would stand to earn 100-150 bn dollars a year if all existing trade barriers were removed. The Commission proposal to remove EU customs duties on everything but arms for the world's 48 poorest countries is therefore a small step in the right direction. According to reports in the Swedish press, however, it appears that the Commission is considering keeping customs duties on agricultural products. That is alarming, as poor countries' exports are concentrated in the sphere of labour-intensive products, especially agricultural produce. It should be borne in mind that, at present, the highest EU duties are precisely on agricultural products and also that nearly half the EU budget is spent on subsidising farming in the European Union.

Is it true that the Commission is considering keeping protective tariffs on agricultural products, thus rendering its proposal practically ineffective?

 
  
 

The Commission’s proposal is, and will remain, to grant free access to Everything But Arms, including sensitive agricultural products originating in least-developed countries.

However, in order to address concerns about the implications of rapid liberalisation of sensitive products, the Commission has recently been reviewing the pace of liberalisation. This may lead to postponing the full liberalisation of imports of rice and sugar until 2009. At the same time, temporary measures are envisaged to provide effective market access for these products as well in the interim period until full liberalisation.

Far from rendering the original Commission proposal ineffective, any such adjustments would ensure immediate benefits to least-developed countries and would also maintain the product coverage proposed, that is, Everything But Arms.

 

Question no 106 by Ewa Hedkvist Petersen (H-0102/01)
 Subject: Children as consumers of TV advertising
 

The Television without Frontiers directive (89/552/EEC)(1) and the directive which amends it (97/36/EC)(2) lay down the ethical rules with which TV advertising must comply. One such rule is that children's gullibility or lack of experience must not be exploited to entice them to buy goods or services. In summer 2000, the Swedish Market Court issued a ruling concerning a TV advertisement for 'Warm Oat Crunch' flakes produced by Quaker Oats. The advertisement took the form of a serial, but the Market Court considered that it was not targeted specifically at children, the reason being that the product can be used both by adults and by children. The ruling thus focused mainly on the product rather than on the advertisement itself. Does the Commission share this product-oriented approach to TV advertising which does not take account of the fact that an advertisement is targeted primarily at children?

 
  
 

The Television without frontiers (TVWF) Directive (Council Directive 89/552/EEC, as amended by Directive 97/36/EC of the Parliament and of the Council) provides the legal framework for television broadcasting within the Community.

The TVWF Directive, as adopted in 1989, contained certain provisions relating to television advertising. In particular, Article 16 lays down certain principles relating to advertising and children. It states that television advertising shall not cause moral or physical detriment to minors and sets out certain criteria to achieve that aim. These criteria are that advertising:

Shall not directly exhort minors to buy a product or service by exploiting their inexperience or credulity;

Shall not directly encourage minors to persuade their parents or others to purchase the goods or services being advertised;

Shall not unreasonably show minors in dangerous situations.

It should be noted that these provisions apply to all advertising, and not simply to advertising intended for minors. The Commission has no reason at this stage to consider that the approach of the Directive on this issue needs to be modified.

When the directive was reviewed, the Commission undertook to carry out a study on the impact of television advertising and teleshopping on minors, in the context of the next review of the Television Directive, in accordance with Article 26 thereof. This study should be completed shortly. A Commission Communication on the study will be transmitted to the Parliament and the Council following the finalisation of the study.

 
 

(1) OJ L 298, 17.10.1989, p. 23
(2) OJ L 202, 30.7.1997, p. 60

 

Question no 107 by John Bowis (H-0103/01)
 Subject: Packaging Directive
 

Is the Commission aware of the ruling in the British High Court that, under the European Packaging Directive, a plastic flowerpot is deemed to be part of the packaging and not an intrinsic part of growing a plant? What was the intention in this respect and will the Commission bring forward immediate steps to correct this absurd anomaly?

 
  
 

The Commission is aware of the ruling in the British High Court that plant pots are to be considered as packaging according to the national legislation in the United Kingdom.

However, the Commission can neither interfere with the ruling of the High Court, nor provide a definitive interpretation of the Community legislation. The definition of packaging, as stated in Directive 94/62/EC(1)1 on packaging and packaging waste, may only be determined ultimately by the Court of Justice.

The Commission recognises that the definition of packaging as stated in Council Directive 94/62/EC has proven difficult to interpret in certain specific cases, such as flower pots, and that differing interpretations of the concept of packaging could lead to market distortion. For that reason, the Commission has been working on the issue, inter alia, with experts of Member States.

It seems that a consensus has emerged from these discussions that it could be useful to complement the current definition by annexing technical guidance to the Directive. This technical guidance might be based on the results of exchange of opinions conducted within the framework of a structured dialogue between all the different stakeholders of the packaging chain – Member States, industry, environmental non governmental organisations (NGOs) and consumers.

 
 

(1)1 OJ L 365, 31.12.1994, p. 10-23

 

Question no 108 by Caroline Lucas (H-0104/01)
 Subject: Testing on animals
 

The possibility that a new European chemicals policy might require a large programme of testing on animals is a cause of grave concern to very many people in Europe.

The current system requires that a standard set of tests is completed before any safety assessment is carried out, and lacks a clear strategy for minimising animal testing. Would the Commission agree that a new system is required that is based on a step-wise approach, so that tests for properties with the most significant risk management implications are conducted first, avoiding inconsequential animal testing? Furthermore, would it not agree that before any additional animal tests are considered, full use should be made of all existing information, including evidence of safety-in-use (which may cover many years of use by the public), structure-activity analysis and other non-animal test methods and that wherever appropriate, the precautionary principle should be rigorously applied?

 
  
 

The Commission is indeed considering the issue of the safety of chemical substances. It is particularly concerned that the majority of the chemical substances now on the Community market were first marketed before 1981, when the requirement for fuller information on toxicological and ecotoxicological properties was introduced for new chemicals. Therefore, the market is dominated by substances for which there is no or insufficient data to demonstrate their safety. This situation should be improved, as serious damage to human health has already occurred in the case of asbestos where workers were exposed.

Against this background, it is necessary to consider how to improve the protection of human health and the environment. In so doing, the Commission will balance the need for more information against its commitment to limit animal testing. Where appropriate, the necessary test programme would have to be minimised by promoting use of the approaches proposed by the honourable Member. Furthermore, test requirements may be reduced for substances neither used by the general population nor released into the environment. The precautionary principle will, of course, be applied as appropriate.

The use of animals in the resulting test programme should be minimised through use of existing alternative testing methods and continuing development of new alternative methods. The Commission is committed to the objective of refinement, reduction and replacement of animal testings as is shown in its research programmes, in particular the Quality of Life Programme, and in the activities carried out by the Joint Research Centre, specifically at the European Centre for the Validation of Alternative Methods which validates alternative methods. This is supported by Community legislation requiring that, where equivalent methods exist, using the minimum number of animals shall be chosen. The forthcoming White Paper on chemical strategy will also reflect this priority.

The honourable Member will see that the Commission is seeking to balance animal welfare considerations against the potentially serious threats to human health and the environment, including wild animals, presented by insufficiently tested chemicals.

 

Question no 109 by Gerard Collins (H-0107/01)
 Subject: Policies for active ageing
 

Will the Commission outline the measures being taken by the Member States to develop policies for active ageing with the aim of enhancing the capacity of and incentives for older workers to remain in the labour force as long as possible and will it indicate whether particular steps are being taken to introduce flexible working arrangements, ensure older workers have appropriate access to education and training and whether a real effort is being made to remove disincentives for older workers so that they can remain active in the labour market?

 
  
 

The Commission has two sources of information on what Member States are doing to improve the opportunities for older workers in work places and labour markets: (1) occasional studies and (2) the ongoing monitoring of National Action Plans as part of the employment process.

(1) A recent comprehensive overview of measures taken by individual Member States was produced by the Employment Observatory and published as Trends No. 33 "Older Workers on the Labour Market" in 1999.

(2) In the European employment strategy, attention to older workers' issues has increased considerably over the last 2 years. From a simple mention in the 1999 employment guidelines, older workers issues have been upgraded to a special guideline (no. 3) on active ageing in 2001. At the same time a number of other guidelines are of relevance for the improvement of conditions for older workers (e.g. 4-6 life long learning; 8 anti-discrimination; 14 flexible working time etc.).

In its employment recommendations the Commission and the Council have paid considerable attention to older workers issues both in 2000 and in 2001. It was suggested to 8 Member States in 2000 and to 9 of them in 2001 that they ought to intensify their efforts to enhance the capacity and incentives for older workers to remain in the labour market.

The "Joint Employment Report" assesses each year progress achieved by Member States in implementing the Guidelines. The 2000 Joint Report recognised that: "Most Member States have started to implement or are planning to introduce soon measures encouraging older people to stay longer in employment, by raising the retirement age or by introducing or strengthening disincentives to early retirement. The most comprehensive reforms are being undertaken or envisaged in Denmark, Germany, the Netherlands, Austria and Finland."(1)1

At the same time there is room for improvement in the policy attention to older workers’ issues in several Member States. The Commission therefore intends to continue to monitor developments closely and to work with Member States to help them improve the abilities and motivations of older workers to remain in the employed labour force.

 
 

(1)1 Section 3.1.3, page 33

 

Question no 110 by Brian Crowley (H-0109/01)
 Subject: Full employment and the knowledge-based economy
 

The Commission President, Mr Prodi, has stated that one of his key objectives is to create the conditions for full employment by making Europe the world’s most dynamic knowledge-based economy. Will the Commission state how it intends to achieve this objective, where the difficulties lie, and how this objective will be made relevant to young people and the long-term unemployed throughout Europe?

 
  
 

Over the last decade a favourable economic outlook for the Union has been created as a result of a sound macroeconomic framework combined with a real commitment to reform of labour, capital, goods and service markets. However, in a world that does not stand still, progress towards meeting the objectives made at the Lisbon European Council needs to be based on strong commitment to concerted action across different policy domains.

The Lisbon objective requires action both by the Community and the Member States. The combination of policies which is required is a broad one: sound macroeconomic policy; structural reforms which promote adaptable labour markets, increased innovation and higher competitiveness; active policies promoting the development of human resources, lifelong learning, inclusion and solidarity. The Luxembourg process through which the Employment Guidelines are coordinated contributes to ensuring that a strategic and coordinated approach is pursued, and several of the individual Guidelines are specifically aimed at young people and the long-term unemployed.

Along the road to meeting the Lisbon objective, certain themes are of particular importance. First, taking account of job quality while increasing employment rates. Second, providing life-long learning which enhances the capacity of people to participation in the knowledge-based society. Third, ensuring that all relevant stakeholders play their part: from governments, to Social Partners, and Regional and Local actors. Fourth, taking care that different policy actions are consistent and complementary. Last, establishing methods for measuring actions and progress. In each of these areas, care is being taken to ensure that the interests of young people and the long-term unemployed are being taken into account. In particular, it should be noted that the newly established Social Inclusion process promises to further enhance this positive situation.

 

Question no 111 by Niall Andrews (H-0111/01)
 Subject: The homeless
 

Given the interest shown by the European Parliament over a long period of time in tackling homelessness, can the Commission outline how it intends to support the Member States and NGOs in their efforts in this area in 2001?

 
  
 

The Lisbon European Council recognised that the number of people living below the poverty line and in social exclusion in the Community is unacceptable and that efforts are needed to make a significant impact on the eradication of poverty and exclusion in the next 10 years. Homelessness, including other forms of precarious housing, is an important dimension of poverty and social exclusion.

In order to strengthen the fight against social exclusion in the Community, the Lisbon European Council also agreed for the Member States to work together through an open method of coordination, combining national action plans on how Member States are fighting social exclusion and a Community Programme (to facilitate policy-oriented cooperation in this field between Member States). The aim of the national action plans and the Programme is to make it possible to achieve the common objectives against poverty and social exclusion which were adopted last December by the Nice European Council. Among these objectives, several are particularly relevant for tackling homelessness.

The National action plans will be submitted by June 2001 for the period 2001-2003. They should include concrete initiatives aimed at tackling and preventing homelessness. The Council and the Commission will subsequently adopt a joint report by the end of 2001 presenting the conclusion of the examination of the plans.

The Community programme, which should support transnational cooperation activities, is currently discussed in Parliament and the Council under the codecision procedure. The Swedish Presidency of the Council has expressed hopes for the Programme to be adopted in the summer of this year. The start of the programme does, of course, depend on the date of adoption by the Council and the Parliament but is most likely to be 1 January 2002. Policy cooperation initiatives on homelessness issues that arise as a (common) priority in the National action plans would be eligible for funding under the programme.

The proposed Programme includes a provision to support networking at European level of organisations involved with the fight against poverty and social exclusion. The Commission is currently supporting the FEANTSA network which focuses on fighting homelessness and will endeavour to do so until the start of the programme.

 

Question no 112 by James (Jim) Fitzsimons (H-0114/01)
 Subject: Japanese/Russian plans to ship nuclear waste via the Artic
 

Is the Commission aware of negotiations between Japan and Russia to ship radioactive waste from Europe to Japan via the Arctic from 2002 in order to circumvent widespread international opposition to shipments of radioactive nuclear waste and is there any action the Commission can take to halt such shipments which would have to be routed via the Irish Sea or the west of Ireland to reach the Atlantic or, alternatively, through the English Channel and the North Sea?

 
  
 

The transport of radioactive waste by sea or land is an operation which is fully controlled at a technical level. It is a routine operation which has been carried out for several decades now, whether within one country or between countries, without any unfortunate incidents such as leaks or other damage. The radioactive waste shipped has to meet a number of strict criteria, in particular concerning container characteristics, as defined by the International Atomic Energy Agency and incorporated into national law. There are also various international agreements concerning accidents, safety and transport in the sphere of the transportation of nuclear material.

In the case of maritime shipment, the containers used to transport spent nuclear fuel have been designed to be totally resistant in the event of immersion, even at great depths. In the present case, the only potential problem concerns adaptation of the specifications of the transporting vessels – which have to operate in Arctic waters – to International Maritime Organisation standards. Only that organisation has the authority to issue binding guidelines on the matter.

The fact that the transport of radioactive waste between Europe and Japan is carried out by sea, across the Arctic Ocean and close to the coastline of the Russian Federation, is not in itself grounds for taking preventive action, unless it is found that this transport is infringing IMO rules.

There is no legal instrument which would enable the Commission to restrict the use of Arctic waters for transportation of radioactive waste produced by the reprocessing of nuclear fuel in Europe.

 

Question no 113 by Efstratios Korakas (H-0116/01)
 Subject: Unjustified delays in the implementation of regulations
 

Article 56 of Regulation (EC) 1257/1999(1) on support for rural development from the EAGGF provides that this Regulation shall apply in relation to Community support as from 1 January 2000. Article 50 of Regulation (EC) 1750/1999(2) laying down detailed rules for the application of the previous Regulation (EC) 1257/1999 contains the same provision. Even though over a year has elapsed since the supposed date of implementation of these two regulations, in Greece and maybe also in other countries they have not yet been implemented, at least as far as young farmers are concerned. This has caused enormous problems for young people wishing to take up farming.

Will the Commission say why the above regulations have not been implemented as from 1 January 2000, as they should have been?

 
  
 

The honourable Member is correct in stating that Regulation (EC) No 1257/1999 on support for rural development shall apply as of 1 January 2000.

This date marks the point where projects become eligible to receive aid under the ‘rural development’ operational programme submitted by Greece in April 2000 and which also includes measures in favour of young farmers.

However, Member States are very often reluctant to implement programmes until they are sure of the Commission’s approval and indeed, this programme has not yet been approved by the Commission.

One of the reasons for this delay, which the Commission is well aware of, is the operational status of the new management authorities required following the reform of the Structural Funds. In liaison with the Greek Authorities, the Commission has drawn up a methodology and a timetable for the implementation of these management authorities, which should have been allocated sufficient personnel and which should be fully operational within the first two months of 2001. This would make it possible for the programme to be officially adopted in March and subsequently for measures to be implemented from that time on. The Greek Government has just informed the Commission that the management authority responsible for the Rural Development programme shall be operational by the end of February 2001. This programme should therefore be approved shortly by the Commission.

Nonetheless, the Commission would like to assure the honourable Member that Commission Regulation No 2075/2000, amending Regulation No 1750/1999, stipulates that, as regards applications for aid made by young farmers for the first time in 2000, one of the criteria – the requirement of being under 40 years of age – must be fulfilled at the time of presenting the application and not at the time that a decision is made to grant aid in that specific case. This interim provision will make it possible to partly offset the effects of the above delay affecting young farmers who submitted an application for aid before first setting up business during that year.

 
 

(1) OJ L 160, 26.6.1999, p. 80.
(2) OJ L 214, 13.8.1999, p. 31.

 

Question no 114 by Lone Dybkjær (H-0118/01)
 Subject: Malaria vaccines
 

Congratulations are due to the Commission for the great step made towards developing a new malaria vaccine referred to in the press release of 11 January 2001 'New malaria vaccines – made in Europe'. The Commission has laid the foundations for controlling one of the poverty-related diseases discussed at the round table conference held on 28 September 2000.

What action is the Commission now planning in order to ensure that the development of malaria vaccines continues in Europe and to ensure that the necessary human and institutional resources are available for the development and testing of vaccines in malaria-affected regions, in particular in Africa? What will it do to support pan-African networks for the testing of malaria vaccines, e.g. the AMVTN (African Malaria Vaccine Testing Network)?

Parliament has adopted budget lines B7-6211 and B7-6212 on poverty-related diseases as a means of stressing how important it is that the Commission step up its efforts in this area. What ideas has the Commission formulated regarding the future budgetary structure and financing for continued actions in this area?

 
  
 

The Commission appreciates the support the honourable Members and many other Members have expressed for the Commission’s new policy on Malaria, HIV/AIDS and TB in the context of poverty reduction. The deaths of 5 million people per year, most of them in developing countries, from these three communicable diseases represent a global emergency against which the Commission with the Member States and its international partners should take coherent and concerted action. Although priority must be given to the preventive efforts, increased support for the development and availability of new vaccines for AIDS and malaria will be an integral part of the efforts.

The Commission has prepared a “Programme for Action” to implement the new Community policy on the three major communicable diseases over the coming 5 years (2001-2006) which will be sent to Parliament and Council. It is hoped that Parliament will support the Programme for Action which identifies a substantial number of actions in support of the development and availability of AIDS and malaria vaccines including support to networks for the testing of vaccines, such as the African Malaria Vaccine Testing Network.

The Programme for Action (2001-2006) will be financed through the Community’s existing financial instruments such as the European Development Fund (EDF) and geographical budget lines. Where appropriate, this may include the use of dedicated thematic budget lines such as budget line B7-6213 (previous B7-6211 and B7-6212). In addition, a Communication on additional Community investment in research concerning communicable diseases in developing countries, including malaria vaccines, will be presented by the end of 2001.

 

Question no 115 by Lisbeth Grönfeldt Bergman (H-0119/01)
 Subject: Discrimination against e-tailers
 

A recently published study shows that, in Sweden, suppliers are refusing to supply e-tailers and are charging them higher prices than retailers who do not sell to consumers over the Internet. The reason seems to be that, because they are more cost-effective, e-tailers can keep their prices lower, which gives them a competitive edge over their suppliers' other customers. Consumers are the main losers as a result of such discrimination against e-tailers, since e-tailing enables consumers who live in areas far from big cities and specialist shops to buy goods to which they previously did not have access. They may no longer be able to get hold of the goods they want at all if e-tailing is not given the conditions it needs to survive.

Does the Commission consider such conduct on the part of suppliers to be compatible with EC law and in line with its own endeavours to improve Europe's competitiveness in the new economy?

 
  
 

Competition Law only prohibits producers refusing to supply retailers or charging them different prices in the following instances:

When the pricing policy of the producer is governed by an agreement or a concerted practice made between it and at least one other producer which may affect trade between Member States or

if the producer holds a dominant position on a relevant market within a substantial part of the common market.

If the honourable Member could provide more information on the companies involved, the markets on which they operate, their market shares and the possible effects on trade between Member States, the Commission would be happy to ascertain whether the refusal to deal and price discrimination constitute infringements of Community competition law.

 

Question no 116 by John Walls Cushnahan (H-0121/01)
 Subject: Investment in Irish Regional Airports
 

Are there any EU policies/regulations/rules which would prevent the Irish Government from providing financial aid for improving facilities at Irish regional airports such as terminal and apron development and additional car parking?

 
  
 

In the past the provision of airport capacity has been organised largely as a non-market activity. In that context the Commission has always considered the construction and development of airport infrastructure as being a measure of general policy which cannot be controlled by the Commission under the EC Treaty rules on State aid.(1)1

However, the airport industry has been going through a process of deep transformation for the last years. In the increasingly liberal and competitive environment of air transport, airport operations have also been changing in nature and have been evolving from government branches towards commercial undertakings. A major feature in this evolution is the emerging of a certain degree of competition between airports. This evolution is reflected in a recent judgement of the Court of First Instance(2)2, which clearly states that airports are undertakings submitted to competition law.

Under these circumstances, competition law, namely Articles 87 and 86 of the EC Treaty may apply to airports too. Of course, the application of the relevant provisions of the Treaty to the public financing of airport infrastructure must duly take into account the Community interest as well as the general economic interest airports may be entrusted with.

For these reasons it is difficult to give a general answer to an individual case. A case by case assessment is necessary, in order to evaluate if, in the cases at stake, the provision of financial aid has an impact on competition between airlines and between airports.

 
 

(1)1 See Commission’s Guidelines for the application of Articles 92 and 93 of the EC Treaty and 61 of the EEA Agreement to State aids in the aviation sector, OJ C 350 of 10.12.1994.
(2)2 Judgement of the Court of First Instance of 12 December 2000, in the case T-128/98 Aéroports de Paris vs Commission.

 

Question no 117 by Bárbara Dührkop Dührkop (H-0123/01)
 Subject: European vehicle licence plates
 

Is the Commission aware that the French traffic police are stopping and imposing fines of Ptas 25 000 on Spanish motorists driving in France who have not exchanged their Spanish licence plates for the new European model which was adopted several months ago in Spain? The reason given for the fine is a failure to display the new European licence plate, and has nothing to do with whether or not the vehicle displays the letter denoting its country of origin, which is compulsory under the Vienna Convention.

Given that in Spain all new cars automatically have the new model licence plates, and that older cars may retain the old model or, on payment of a flat­rate fee, may exchange them on a voluntary basis, and since there are no European rules obliging Member States to replace old licence plates with the new model, what are the Commission's views on the attitude of the French authorities described above? Will the Commission take steps to obtain the relevant information and act accordingly?

 
  
 

The requirement to display the distinguishing sign of the country where a vehicle is registered originates in Article 37 of the 1968 Vienna Convention on Road Traffic.

Council Regulation (EC) No 2411/98 provides for the mutual recognition by Member States of the distinguishing sign of the Member State in which motor vehicles are registered provided that these signs conform to the provisions laid down in the annex.

As the honourable Member states, Community law does not impose the use of the so-called Community model licence plate described in that regulation.

There are therefore no grounds for police action against drivers of vehicles purely because they are not using the European model licence plate.

If proof (e.g. copies of police reports) were to be brought to the attention of the Commission confirming that the French authorities are imposing fines in such circumstances, then the Commission would undoubtedly refer the matter immediately to the French authorities.

 

Question no 118 by Konstantinos Alyssandrakis (H-0131/01)
 Subject: Grave threat to citizens' health from increased atmospheric pollution
 

A recent study by the Institute of Public Health Studies in Valencia has confirmed what has been known for years, namely the harmful and indeed fatal consequences of atmospheric pollution for the health of inhabitants of the Planet. 6% of annual deaths are due to atmospheric pollution and, more particularly, to atmospheric pollution caused by the large quantities of sulphur dioxide, nitrogen oxide, carbon monoxide and ozone emitted. It is well established that, apart from industry, exhaust fumes from car motors constitute the main source of pollution. Given that similar studies carried out in Europe have found that atmospheric pollution is responsible not only for cardiovascular diseases but also for respiratory problems, what specific measures does the Commission intend to promote in order effectively to protect citizens' health?

 
  
 

Over the last few years the Community has agreed a substantial package of measures to protect citizens against air pollution. Directive 1999/30/EC sets ambient air quality standards for sulphur dioxide, nitrogen dioxide, particulate matter and lead. A similar Directive, adopted last year, sets standards for carbon monoxide and benzene. All are based on World Health Organisation air quality guidelines. The standards must be met by 2005 or in some cases 2010. But Member States will have to provide the public with information on levels of pollution from the start and must make public plans for improving the worst areas. Proposals setting objectives for ozone, and national emission ceilings to help meet those objectives, are currently before Parliament for Second Reading.

Directive 98/69/EC on light vehicles and Directive 1999/96/EC on Heavy Duty Vehicles are introducing progressively tighter emission limits which will help to meet air quality goals. Directive 97/68/EC is tackling off-road vehicles. Fuel standards are also subject to progressively tighter legislation and the Commission has recently completed a consultation exercise on lowering the sulphur content in fuels to below 10 parts per million.

Member States are implementing the IPPC Directive, amongst others, to reduce emissions from industry. And the proposed revision to the Directive on Large Combustion Plants, also at Second Reading, will further reduce emissions of both sulphur dioxide and nitrogen oxides.

But there is still more to be done. The proposal for a Sixth Environmental Action Programme explains that the Commission intends to set up an integrated thematic programme to develop future policy on air quality. The Clean Air For Europe Programme will aim to ensure that existing legislation is fully implemented, and will review the latest research on health effects and other matters to determine whether standards need to be revised and if so, how they could be met cost effectively. The Commission will present a Communication to Parliament and Council this session, setting out in more detail the aims and scope of the CAFE programme. It is aimed to produce a first integrated strategy by the end of 2004.
